<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saslong_udu_da_Sacun_ora.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Saslong_udu_da_Sacun_ora.jpg/250px-Saslong_udu_da_Sacun_ora.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Saslong_udu_da_Sacun_ora.jpg/375px-Saslong_udu_da_Sacun_ora.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Saslong_udu_da_Sacun_ora.jpg/500px-Saslong_udu_da_Sacun_ora.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saslong_udu_da_Sacun_ora.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Dolomites" title="Dolomites">Dolomites</a> (Italy) are a <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_Italy" title="List of World Heritage Sites in Italy">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Alps</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> are the highest and most extensive <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">mountain range</a> system that lies entirely in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[2]</a></sup> stretching approximately 1,200Â km (750Â mi) across seven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpine_countries" title="Alpine countries">Alpine countries</a> (from west to east): <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[c]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Alpine arch generally extends from <a href="/wiki/Nice" title="Nice">Nice</a> on the western Mediterranean to <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a> on the Adriatic and <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Pannonian_Basin" title="Pannonian Basin">Pannonian Basin</a>. The mountains were formed over tens of millions of years as the African and Eurasian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plates</a> collided. Extreme shortening  caused by the event resulted in marine <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a> rising by <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">thrusting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folding</a> into high mountain peaks such as <a href="/wiki/Mont_Blanc" title="Mont Blanc">Mont Blanc</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Matterhorn" title="Matterhorn">Matterhorn</a>. 
</p><p>Mont Blanc spans the Frenchâ€“Italian border, and at 4,809Â m (15,778Â ft) is the highest mountain in the Alps. The Alpine region area contains 128 peaks higher than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Alpine_four-thousanders" title="List of Alpine four-thousanders">4,000Â m (13,000Â ft)</a>.
</p><p>The altitude and size of the range affect the climate in Europe; in the mountains, precipitation levels vary greatly and climatic conditions consist of distinct zones. Wildlife such as <a href="/wiki/Ibex" title="Ibex">ibex</a> live in the higher peaks to elevations of 3,400Â m (11,155Â ft), and plants such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edelweiss" title="Edelweiss">edelweiss</a> grow in rocky areas in lower elevations as well as in higher elevations. 
</p><p>Evidence of human habitation in the Alps goes back to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeolithic" title="Palaeolithic">Palaeolithic</a> era. A <a href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi" title="Ã–tzi">mummified man</a>, determined to be 5,000 years old, was discovered on a glacier at the Austrianâ€“Italian border in 1991.
</p><p>By the 6th century BC, the Celtic <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La TÃ¨ne culture">La TÃ¨ne culture</a> was well established. <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> notably crossed the Alps with a herd of elephants, and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> had settlements in the region. In 1800, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> crossed one of the mountain passes with an army of 40,000. The 18th and 19th centuries saw an influx of naturalists, writers, and artists, in particular, the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantics</a>, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_alpinism" title="Golden age of alpinism">golden age of alpinism</a> as mountaineers began to ascend the peaks.
</p><p>The Alpine region has a strong cultural identity. The traditional culture of farming, cheesemaking, and woodworking still exists in Alpine villages, although the tourist industry began to grow early in the 20th century and expanded greatly after World War II to become the dominant industry by the end of the century. 
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Winter_Olympic_Games" title="Winter Olympic Games">Winter Olympic Games</a> have been hosted in the Swiss, French, Italian, Austrian and German Alps. As of 2010, the region is home to 14Â million people and has 120Â million annual visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatrÃ©8_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChatrÃ©8-8">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_toponymy">Etymology and toponymy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and toponymy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Albion#Etymology" title="Albion">Albion Â§Â Etymology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bildstock_an_der_Alpe_Bardughe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Bildstock_an_der_Alpe_Bardughe.jpg/220px-Bildstock_an_der_Alpe_Bardughe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Bildstock_an_der_Alpe_Bardughe.jpg/330px-Bildstock_an_der_Alpe_Bardughe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Bildstock_an_der_Alpe_Bardughe.jpg/440px-Bildstock_an_der_Alpe_Bardughe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bildstock_an_der_Alpe_Bardughe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An "Alp" refers to a high elevation pasture frequented only in summer. It often includes several huts and small places of worship (here the Alpe BardughÃ¨ in <a href="/wiki/Ticino" title="Ticino">Ticino</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>The English word <i>Alps</i> comes from the Latin <i>Alpes</i>.
</p><p>The Latin word <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/en:Alpes" title="wikt:en:Alpes">Alpes</a></i> could possibly come from the adjective <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/en:albus" title="wikt:en:albus">albus</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline.com_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline.com-9">[6]</a></sup> ("white"), or could possibly come from the Greek goddess <a href="/wiki/Alphito" title="Alphito">Alphito</a>, whose name is related to <i>alphita</i>, the "white flour"; <i><a href="/wiki/Alphos" title="Alphos">alphos</a></i>, a dull white leprosy; and finally the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> word <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%92%80%A0%F0%92%89%BA%F0%92%80%B8#Hittite" title="wikt:ð’€ ð’‰ºð’€¸">*albÊ°Ã³s</a>. Similarly, the river god <a href="/wiki/Alpheus_(deity)" title="Alpheus (deity)">Alpheus</a> is also supposed to derive from the Greek <i>alphos</i> and means whitish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his commentary on the <i>Aeneid</i> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vergil" title="Vergil">Vergil</a>, the late fourth-century grammarian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maurus_Servius_Honoratus" title="Maurus Servius Honoratus">Maurus Servius Honoratus</a> says that all high mountains are called <i>Alpes</i> by Celts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i>Oxford English Dictionary</i>, the Latin <i>Alpes</i> might derive from a <a href="/wiki/Pre-Indo-European_languages" title="Pre-Indo-European languages">pre-Indo-European</a> word *<i>alb</i> "hill"; "Albania" is a related derivation. Albania, a name not native to the region known as the country of <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, has been used as a name for several mountainous areas across Europe. 
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman times</a>, "Albania" was a name for the eastern <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, while in the English languages "Albania" (or "Albany") was occasionally used as a name for <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[9]</a></sup> although it is more likely derived from the Latin word <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/en:albus" title="wikt:en:albus">albus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline.com_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline.com-9">[6]</a></sup> the color white.
</p><p>In modern languages the term <i>alp</i>, <i>alm</i>, <i>albe</i> or <i>alpe</i> refers to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transhumance_in_the_Alps" title="Transhumance in the Alps">grazing pastures</a> in the alpine regions below the glaciers, not the peaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid93_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid93-13">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>An <i>alp</i> refers to a high mountain pasture, typically near or above the <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a>, where cows and other livestock are taken to be grazed during the summer months and where huts and hay barns can be found, sometimes constituting tiny hamlets. Therefore, the term "the Alps", as a reference to the mountains, is a misnomer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds43ff_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds43ff-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleming_4_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming_4-15">[12]</a></sup> The term for the mountain peaks varies by nation and language: words such as <i>Horn</i>, <i>Kogel</i>, <i>Kopf</i>, <i>Gipfel</i>, <i>Spitze</i>, <i>Stock</i>, and <i>Berg</i> are used in German-speaking regions; <i>Mont</i>, <i>Pic</i>, <i>TÃªte</i>, <i>Pointe</i>, <i>Dent</i>, <i>Roche</i>, and <i>Aiguille</i> in French-speaking regions; and <i>Monte</i>, <i>Picco</i>, <i>Corno</i>, <i>Punta</i>, <i>Pizzo</i>, or <i>Cima</i> in Italian-speaking regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_the_Alps" title="Geography of the Alps">Geography of the Alps</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alps_with_borders.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Alps_with_borders.jpg/260px-Alps_with_borders.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Alps_with_borders.jpg/390px-Alps_with_borders.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Alps_with_borders.jpg/520px-Alps_with_borders.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alps_with_borders.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Alps extend in an arc from France in the south and west to Slovenia in the east, and from Monaco in the south to Germany in the north.</div></div></div>
<p>The Alps are a crescent shaped geographic feature of central Europe that ranges in an 800Â km (500Â mi) arc (curved line) from east to west and is 200Â km (120Â mi) in width. The mean height of the mountain peaks is 2.5Â km (1.6Â mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceben_22â€“24_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ceben_22â€“24-17">[14]</a></sup> The range stretches from the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> north above the <a href="/wiki/Po_(river)" title="Po (river)">Po</a> basin, extending through France from <a href="/wiki/Grenoble" title="Grenoble">Grenoble</a>, and stretching eastward through mid and southern Switzerland. The range continues onward toward <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, Austria, and east to the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatrÃ©9_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChatrÃ©9-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beattie_xiiâ€“xiii_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beattie_xiiâ€“xiii-20">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>To the south it dips into northern Italy and to the north extends to the southern border of <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beattie_xiiâ€“xiii_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beattie_xiiâ€“xiii-20">[17]</a></sup> In areas like <a href="/wiki/Chiasso" title="Chiasso">Chiasso</a>, Switzerland, and <a href="/wiki/Allg%C3%A4u" title="AllgÃ¤u">AllgÃ¤u</a>, Bavaria, the demarcation between the mountain range and the flatlands are clear; in other places such as <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, the demarcation is less clear.
</p><p>The countries with the greatest alpine territory are Austria (28.7% of the total area), Italy (27.2%), France (21.4%) and Switzerland (13.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2016_1129_KL1556_Monte_Rosa_Matterhorn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/2016_1129_KL1556_Monte_Rosa_Matterhorn.jpg/220px-2016_1129_KL1556_Monte_Rosa_Matterhorn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/2016_1129_KL1556_Monte_Rosa_Matterhorn.jpg/330px-2016_1129_KL1556_Monte_Rosa_Matterhorn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/2016_1129_KL1556_Monte_Rosa_Matterhorn.jpg/440px-2016_1129_KL1556_Monte_Rosa_Matterhorn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2016_1129_KL1556_Monte_Rosa_Matterhorn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of the <a href="/wiki/Pennine_Alps" title="Pennine Alps">Pennine Alps</a>, the second-highest range of the Alps</div></div></div>
<p>The highest portion of the range is divided by the glacial trough of the <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="RhÃ´ne">RhÃ´ne</a> valley, from <a href="/wiki/Mont_Blanc" title="Mont Blanc">Mont Blanc</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Matterhorn" title="Matterhorn">Matterhorn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monte_Rosa" title="Monte Rosa">Monte Rosa</a> on the southern side, and the <a href="/wiki/Bernese_Alps" title="Bernese Alps">Bernese Alps</a> on the northern. The peaks in the easterly portion of the range, in Austria and Slovenia, are smaller than those in the central and western portions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beattie_xiiâ€“xiii_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Beattie_xiiâ€“xiii-20">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The variances in nomenclature in the region spanned by the Alps make classification of the mountains and subregions difficult, but a general classification is that of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Alps" title="Eastern Alps">Eastern Alps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Alps" title="Western Alps">Western Alps</a> with the divide between the two occurring in eastern Switzerland according to geologist Stefan Schmid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid93_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid93-13">[10]</a></sup> near the <a href="/wiki/Spl%C3%BCgen_Pass" title="SplÃ¼gen Pass">SplÃ¼gen Pass</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lanersbach.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4240" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lanersbach.jpg/220px-Lanersbach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lanersbach.jpg/330px-Lanersbach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lanersbach.jpg/440px-Lanersbach.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lanersbach.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical alpine village in the <a href="/wiki/Tuxertal" title="Tuxertal">Tuxertal</a> valley of <a href="/wiki/Tyrol_(state)" title="Tyrol (state)">Tyrol</a>, Austria</div></div></div>
<p>The highest peaks of the Western Alps and Eastern Alps, respectively, are Mont Blanc, at 4,810Â m (15,780Â ft)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Piz_Bernina" title="Piz Bernina">Piz Bernina</a> at 4,049Â m (13,284Â ft). The second-highest major peaks are <a href="/wiki/Monte_Rosa" title="Monte Rosa">Monte Rosa</a> at 4,634Â m (15,203Â ft) and <a href="/wiki/Ortler" title="Ortler">Ortler</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> at 3,905Â m (12,810Â ft), respectively.
</p><p>A Series of lower mountain ranges run parallel to the main chain of the Alps, including the <a href="/wiki/French_Prealps" title="French Prealps">French Prealps</a> in France and the <a href="/wiki/Jura_Mountains" title="Jura Mountains">Jura Mountains</a> in Switzerland and France. The secondary chain of the Alps follows the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_divide" title="Drainage divide">watershed</a> from the Mediterranean Sea to the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Woods" title="Vienna Woods">Wienerwald</a>, passing over many of the highest and most well-known peaks in the Alps. 
From the Colle di Cadibona to <a href="/wiki/Col_de_Tende" title="Col de Tende">Col de Tende</a> it runs westwards, before turning to the northwest and then, near the <a href="/wiki/Maddalena_Pass" title="Maddalena Pass">Colle della Maddalena</a>, to the north. Upon reaching the Swiss border, the line of the main chain heads approximately east-northeast, a heading it follows until its end near Vienna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The northeast end of the Alpine arc directly on the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, which flows into the Black Sea, is the <a href="/wiki/Leopoldsberg" title="Leopoldsberg">Leopoldsberg</a> near Vienna. In contrast, the southeastern part of the Alps ends on the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a> in the area around Trieste towards <a href="/wiki/Duino" title="Duino">Duino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barcola" title="Barcola">Barcola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Passes">Passes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Passes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Principal_passes_of_the_Alps" title="Principal passes of the Alps">Principal passes of the Alps</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andermatt-Teufelsbruecke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Andermatt-Teufelsbruecke.jpg/220px-Andermatt-Teufelsbruecke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Andermatt-Teufelsbruecke.jpg/330px-Andermatt-Teufelsbruecke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Andermatt-Teufelsbruecke.jpg/440px-Andermatt-Teufelsbruecke.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andermatt-Teufelsbruecke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teufelsbr%C3%BCcke" title="TeufelsbrÃ¼cke">TeufelsbrÃ¼cke</a></i> (Devil's Bridge) on the route to the <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Pass" title="Gotthard Pass">Gotthard Pass</a>; the currently used bridge from 1958 over the first drivable bridge from 1830</div></div></div>
<p>The Alps have been crossed for war and commerce, and by pilgrims, students and tourists. Crossing routes by road, train, or foot are known as <i>passes</i>, and usually consist of depressions in the mountains in which a valley leads from the plains and hilly pre-mountainous zones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidgeLakeKnox1911740_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidgeLakeKnox1911740-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the medieval period <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hospices" title="Hospices">hospices</a> were established by religious orders at the summits of many of the main passes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleming_4_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming_4-15">[12]</a></sup> The most important passes are the <a href="/wiki/Col_de_l%27Iseran" title="Col de l'Iseran">Col de l'Iseran</a> (the highest), the <a href="/wiki/Col_Agnel" title="Col Agnel">Col Agnel</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Brenner_Pass" title="Brenner Pass">Brenner Pass</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mont-Cenis" title="Mont-Cenis">Mont-Cenis</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_St._Bernard_Pass" title="Great St. Bernard Pass">Great St. Bernard Pass</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Col_de_Tende" title="Col de Tende">Col de Tende</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Pass" title="Gotthard Pass">Gotthard Pass</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Semmering_Pass" title="Semmering Pass">Semmering Pass</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Simplon_Pass" title="Simplon Pass">Simplon Pass</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Stelvio_Pass" title="Stelvio Pass">Stelvio Pass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Crossing the Italian-Austrian border, the Brenner Pass separates the <a href="/wiki/%C3%96tztal_Alps" title="Ã–tztal Alps">Ã–tztal Alps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zillertal_Alps" title="Zillertal Alps">Zillertal Alps</a> and has been in use as a trading route since the 14th century. The lowest of the Alpine passes at 985Â m (3,232Â ft), the Semmering crosses from <a href="/wiki/Lower_Austria" title="Lower Austria">Lower Austria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Styria" title="Styria">Styria</a>; since the 12th century when a hospice was built there, it has seen continuous use. A railroad with a tunnel 1.6Â km (1Â mi) long was built along the route of the pass in the mid-19th century. With a summit of 2,469Â m (8,100Â ft), the Great St. Bernard Pass is one of the highest in the Alps, crossing the Italian-Swiss border east of the Pennine Alps along the flanks of Mont Blanc. The pass was used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> to cross 40,000 troops in 1800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-switz_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-switz-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:758px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Versant_sud_val_cenis.jpg" title="The col du Mont-Cenis (2,081Â m (6,827Â ft)) at the centre left of the picture gives access to a large alpine lake, and further away to the Italian peninsula 12 kilometres (7.5Â mi) beyond the pass."><img alt="" data-file-height="2936" data-file-width="11856" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Versant_sud_val_cenis.jpg/750px-Versant_sud_val_cenis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Versant_sud_val_cenis.jpg/1125px-Versant_sud_val_cenis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Versant_sud_val_cenis.jpg/1500px-Versant_sud_val_cenis.jpg 2x" width="750"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Versant_sud_val_cenis.jpg" title="File:Versant sud val cenis.jpg"> </a></div>The col du Mont-Cenis (2,081Â m (6,827Â ft)) at the centre left of the picture gives access to a large alpine lake, and further away to the Italian peninsula 12 kilometres (7.5Â mi) beyond the pass.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mont_Cenis" title="Mont Cenis">Mont Cenis</a> pass has been a major commercial and military road between Western Europe and Italy. The pass was crossed by many troops on their way to the Italian peninsula. From <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I_(emperor)" title="Constantine I (emperor)">Constantine I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> to <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry IV</a>, NapolÃ©on and more recently the German <a href="/wiki/Gebirgsj%C3%A4ger" title="GebirgsjÃ¤ger">GebirgsjÃ¤gers</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Now the pass has been supplanted by the <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9jus_Road_Tunnel" title="FrÃ©jus Road Tunnel">FrÃ©jus Highway Tunnel</a> (opened 1980) and <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9jus_Rail_Tunnel" title="FrÃ©jus Rail Tunnel">Rail Tunnel</a> (opened 1871).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Saint Gotthard Pass crosses from <a href="/wiki/Central_Switzerland" title="Central Switzerland">Central Switzerland</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ticino" title="Ticino">Ticino</a>; in 1882 the 15Â km-long (9.3Â mi) <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Tunnel" title="Gotthard Tunnel">Saint Gotthard Railway Tunnel</a> was opened connecting <a href="/wiki/Lucerne" title="Lucerne">Lucerne</a> in Switzerland, with <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> in Italy. 98 years later followed <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Road_Tunnel" title="Gotthard Road Tunnel">Gotthard Road Tunnel</a> (16.9Â km (10.5Â mi) long) connecting the <a href="/wiki/A2_motorway_(Switzerland)" title="A2 motorway (Switzerland)">A2 motorway</a> in <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6schenen" title="GÃ¶schenen">GÃ¶schenen</a> on the north side with <a href="/wiki/Airolo" title="Airolo">Airolo</a> on the south side, exactly like the railway tunnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 1 June 2016 the world's longest railway tunnel, the <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Base_Tunnel" title="Gotthard Base Tunnel">Gotthard Base Tunnel</a> was opened, which connects <a href="/wiki/Erstfeld" title="Erstfeld">Erstfeld</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Uri" title="Canton of Uri">canton of Uri</a> with <a href="/wiki/Bodio" title="Bodio">Bodio</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canton_of_Ticino" title="Canton of Ticino">canton of Ticino</a> by two single tubes of 57.1Â km (35.5Â mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is the first tunnel that traverses the Alps on a flat route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 11 December 2016, it has been part of the regular railway timetable and used hourly as standard ride between <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>/<a href="/wiki/Lucerne" title="Lucerne">Lucerne</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zurich" title="Zurich">Zurich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bellinzona" title="Bellinzona">Bellinzona</a>/<a href="/wiki/Lugano" title="Lugano">Lugano</a>/<a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The highest pass in the alps is the <a href="/wiki/Col_de_l%27Iseran" title="Col de l'Iseran">Col de l'Iseran</a> in <a href="/wiki/Savoy" title="Savoy">Savoy</a> (France) at 2,770Â m (9,088Â ft), followed by the Stelvio Pass in northern Italy at 2,756Â m (9,042Â ft); the road was built in the 1820s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Highest_mountains">Highest mountains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Highest mountains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Alpine_four-thousanders" title="List of Alpine four-thousanders">List of Alpine four-thousanders</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A4nnlichen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="351" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/M%C3%A4nnlichen.jpg/330px-M%C3%A4nnlichen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/M%C3%A4nnlichen.jpg/495px-M%C3%A4nnlichen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/M%C3%A4nnlichen.jpg/660px-M%C3%A4nnlichen.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A4nnlichen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Eiger" title="Eiger">Eiger</a> (shown along with the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6nch" title="MÃ¶nch">MÃ¶nch</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jungfrau" title="Jungfrau">Jungfrau</a>) has the tallest north face in the Alps.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Internationale_des_Associations_d%27Alpinisme" title="Union Internationale des Associations d'Alpinisme">Union Internationale des Associations d'Alpinisme</a> (UIAA) has defined a list of 82 "official" Alpine summits that reach at least 4,000Â m (13,123Â ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup> The list includes not only mountains, but also subpeaks with little <a href="/wiki/Topographic_prominence" title="Topographic prominence">prominence</a> that are considered important <a href="/wiki/Mountaineering" title="Mountaineering">mountaineering</a> objectives. Below are listed the 29 "four-thousanders" with at least 300Â m (984Â ft) of prominence.
</p><p>While Mont Blanc was first climbed in 1786 and the Jungfrau in 1811, most of the Alpine four-thousanders were climbed during the second half of the 19th century, notably <a href="/wiki/Piz_Bernina" title="Piz Bernina">Piz Bernina</a> (1850), the <a href="/wiki/Dom_(mountain)" title="Dom (mountain)">Dom</a> (1858), the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Combin" title="Grand Combin">Grand Combin</a> (1859), the <a href="/wiki/Weisshorn" title="Weisshorn">Weisshorn</a> (1861) and the <a href="/wiki/Barre_des_%C3%89crins" title="Barre des Ã‰crins">Barre des Ã‰crins</a> (1864); the ascent of the Matterhorn in 1865 marked the end of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_alpinism" title="Golden age of alpinism">golden age of alpinism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Karl_Blodig" title="Karl Blodig">Karl Blodig</a> (1859â€“1956) was among the first to successfully climb all the major 4,000Â m peaks. He completed his series of ascents in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> Many of the big Alpine three-thousanders were climbed in the early 19th century, notably the <a href="/wiki/Grossglockner" title="Grossglockner">Grossglockner</a> (1800) and the <a href="/wiki/Ortler" title="Ortler">Ortler</a> (1804), although some of them were climbed only much later, such at <a href="/wiki/Mont_Pelvoux" title="Mont Pelvoux">Mont Pelvoux</a> (1848), <a href="/wiki/Monte_Viso" title="Monte Viso">Monte Viso</a> (1861) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Meije" title="La Meije">La Meije</a> (1877).
</p><p>The first British Mont Blanc ascent by a man was in 1788; the first ascent by a woman was in 1808. By the mid-1850s Swiss mountaineers had ascended most of the peaks and were eagerly sought as mountain guides. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Whymper" title="Edward Whymper">Edward Whymper</a> reached the top of the Matterhorn in 1865 (after seven attempts), and in 1938 the last of the six <a href="/wiki/Great_north_faces_of_the_Alps" title="Great north faces of the Alps">great north faces of the Alps</a> was climbed with the first ascent of the Eiger <i>Nordwand</i> (north face of the Eiger).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff197ff_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff197ff-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology_and_orogeny">Geology and orogeny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Geology and orogeny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Alpine_orogeny" title="Alpine orogeny">Alpine orogeny</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Alps" title="Geology of the Alps">Geology of the Alps</a></div>
<p>Important geological concepts were established as naturalists began studying the rock formations of the Alps in the 18th century. In the mid-19th century, the now-defunct theory of <a href="/wiki/Geosyncline" title="Geosyncline">geosynclines</a> was used to explain the presence of "folded" mountain chains but by the mid-20th century the theory of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a> became widely accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graciansky1ff_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graciansky1ff-39">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nante_d%27arpanaz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="446" data-file-width="710" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/52/Nante_d%27arpanaz.jpg/250px-Nante_d%27arpanaz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/52/Nante_d%27arpanaz.jpg/375px-Nante_d%27arpanaz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/52/Nante_d%27arpanaz.jpg/500px-Nante_d%27arpanaz.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nante_d%27arpanaz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The geologic <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folding</a> seen at the Arpanaz waterfall, shown here in a mid-18th-century drawing, was noted by 18th-century geologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graciansky5_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graciansky5-40">[37]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The formation of the Alps (the <a href="/wiki/Alpine_orogeny" title="Alpine orogeny">Alpine orogeny</a>) was an episodic process that began about 300Â million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> Era the <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaean</a> <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinent</a> consisted of a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plate</a>; it broke into separate plates during the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> Era and the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys</a> sea developed between <a href="/wiki/Laurasia" title="Laurasia">Laurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> Period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graciansky1ff_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graciansky1ff-39">[36]</a></sup> The Tethys was later squeezed between colliding plates causing the formation of mountain ranges called the <a href="/wiki/Alpide_belt" title="Alpide belt">Alpide belt</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> to <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>â€”a process that began at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> and continues into the present. The formation of the Alps was a segment of this orogenic process,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graciansky1ff_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Graciansky1ff-39">[36]</a></sup> caused by the collision between the <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian</a> plates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerrard9_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerrard9-42">[39]</a></sup> that began in the late <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> Period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerrard16_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerrard16-43">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under extreme <a href="/wiki/Compressive_stress" title="Compressive stress">compressive stresses</a> and pressure, marine <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a> were uplifted, creating characteristic recumbent <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folds</a>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Nappe" title="Nappe">nappes</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">thrust faults</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup> As the rising peaks underwent erosion, a layer of marine <a href="/wiki/Flysch" title="Flysch">flysch</a> sediments was deposited in the <a href="/wiki/Foreland_basin" title="Foreland basin">foreland basin</a>, and the sediments became involved in younger nappes (folds) as the orogeny progressed. Coarse sediments from the continual uplift and erosion were later deposited in foreland areas as <a href="/wiki/Molasse" title="Molasse">molasse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerrard9_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gerrard9-42">[39]</a></sup> The molasse regions in Switzerland and Bavaria were well-developed and saw further upthrusting of flysch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid102_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid102-45">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_from_Mangart_MC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2109" data-file-width="6042" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/View_from_Mangart_MC.jpg/330px-View_from_Mangart_MC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/View_from_Mangart_MC.jpg/495px-View_from_Mangart_MC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/View_from_Mangart_MC.jpg/660px-View_from_Mangart_MC.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_from_Mangart_MC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The dolomitic peaks of <a href="/wiki/Triglav_National_Park" title="Triglav National Park">Triglav National Park</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Julian_Alps" title="Julian Alps">Julian Alps</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Alpine orogeny occurred in ongoing cycles through to the Paleogene causing differences in nappe structures, with a late-stage orogeny causing the development of the <a href="/wiki/Jura_Mountains" title="Jura Mountains">Jura Mountains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid97_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid97-46">[43]</a></sup> A series of tectonic events in the Triassic, Jurassic and Cretaceous periods caused different <a href="/wiki/Palaeogeography" title="Palaeogeography">paleogeographic</a> regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid97_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid97-46">[43]</a></sup> The Alps are subdivided by different <a href="/wiki/Lithology" title="Lithology">lithology</a> (rock composition) and nappe structures according to the orogenic events that affected them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid93_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid93-13">[10]</a></sup> The geological subdivision differentiates the Western, Eastern Alps, and Southern Alps: the <a href="/wiki/Helvetic_nappes" title="Helvetic nappes">Helveticum</a> in the north, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penninic_nappes" title="Penninic nappes">Penninicum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austroalpine_nappes" title="Austroalpine nappes">Austroalpine system</a> in the centre and, south of the Periadriatic Seam, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Alps_(geology)" title="Southern Alps (geology)">Southern Alpine system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid99_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid99-47">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Matterhorn_from_Domh%C3%BCtte_-_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1073" data-file-width="1610" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Matterhorn_from_Domh%C3%BCtte_-_2.jpg/250px-Matterhorn_from_Domh%C3%BCtte_-_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Matterhorn_from_Domh%C3%BCtte_-_2.jpg/375px-Matterhorn_from_Domh%C3%BCtte_-_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Matterhorn_from_Domh%C3%BCtte_-_2.jpg/500px-Matterhorn_from_Domh%C3%BCtte_-_2.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Matterhorn_from_Domh%C3%BCtte_-_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Compressed metamorphosed Tethyan sediments and their oceanic basement are sandwiched between the tip of the <a href="/wiki/Matterhorn" title="Matterhorn">Matterhorn</a> (Italian-Swiss border), which consists of gneisses originally part of the African plate, and the base of the peak, which is part of the Eurasian plate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graciansky5_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graciansky5-40">[37]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>According to geologist Stefan Schmid, because the Western Alps underwent a metamorphic event in the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> Era while the Austroalpine peaks underwent an event in the Cretaceous Period, the two areas show distinct differences in nappe formations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid97_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid97-46">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Flysch" title="Flysch">Flysch</a> deposits in the Southern Alps of <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a> probably occurred in the Cretaceous or later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid97_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid97-46">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Peaks in France, Italy and Switzerland lie in the "HouilliÃ¨re zone", which consists of <a href="/wiki/Basement_(geology)" title="Basement (geology)">basement</a> with sediments from the Mesozoic Era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid99_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid99-47">[44]</a></sup> High "massifs" with external sedimentary cover are more common in the Western Alps and were affected by <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a> Period <a href="/wiki/Thin-skinned_deformation" title="Thin-skinned deformation">thin-skinned</a> thrusting whereas the Eastern Alps have comparatively few high peaked massifs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid102_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid102-45">[42]</a></sup> Similarly the peaks in eastern Switzerland extending to western Austria (Helvetic nappes) consist of thin-skinned sedimentary folding that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decollement" title="Decollement">detached</a> from former <a href="/wiki/Basement_(geology)" title="Basement (geology)">basement</a> rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmid103_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid103-48">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In simple terms, the structure of the Alps consists of layers of rock of European, African, and oceanic (Tethyan) origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graciansky29ff_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graciansky29ff-49">[46]</a></sup> The bottom nappe structure is of continental European origin, above which are stacked marine sediment nappes, topped off by nappes derived from the African plate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graciansky31_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graciansky31-50">[47]</a></sup> The Matterhorn is an example of the ongoing orogeny and shows evidence of great folding. The tip of the mountain consists of <a href="/wiki/Gneiss" title="Gneiss">gneisses</a> from the African plate; the base of the peak, below the glaciated area, consists of European basement rock. The sequence of Tethyan marine sediments and their oceanic basement is sandwiched between rock derived from the African and European plates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graciansky5_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Graciansky5-40">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:758px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Savine.JPG" title="Haute Maurienne (Ambin and Vanoise massifs) and its exposed crystalline basement made of high-pressure subduction rocks such as blueschist and metaquartzite (picture taken at 2,400 metres or 7,900 feet)"><img alt="" data-file-height="2352" data-file-width="10800" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Savine.JPG/750px-Savine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Savine.JPG/1125px-Savine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Savine.JPG/1500px-Savine.JPG 2x" width="750"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Savine.JPG" title="File:Savine.JPG"> </a></div><a href="/wiki/Maurienne" title="Maurienne">Haute Maurienne</a> (Ambin and Vanoise massifs) and its exposed crystalline basement made of high-pressure subduction rocks such as <a href="/wiki/Blueschist" title="Blueschist">blueschist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quartzite" title="Quartzite">metaquartzite</a> (picture taken at 2,400 metres or 7,900 feet)</div></div></div><div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>The core regions of the Alpine orogenic belt have been folded and fractured in such a manner that erosion created the characteristic steep vertical peaks of the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Alps" title="Swiss Alps">Swiss Alps</a> that rise seemingly straight out of the foreland areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerrard16_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gerrard16-43">[40]</a></sup> Peaks such as Mont Blanc, the Matterhorn, and high peaks in the Pennine Alps, the BrianÃ§onnais, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hohe_Tauern" title="Hohe Tauern">Hohe Tauern</a> consist of layers of rock from the various orogenies including exposures of basement rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the ever-present geologic instability, earthquakes continue in the Alps to this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> Typically, the largest earthquakes in the alps have been between magnitude 6 and 7 on the Richter scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Minerals">Minerals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Minerals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Alps are a source of minerals that have been mined for thousands of years. In the 8th to 6th centuries, BC during the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt culture</a>, Celtic tribes mined copper; later the Romans mined gold for coins in the <a href="/wiki/Bad_Gastein" title="Bad Gastein">Bad Gastein</a> area. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erzberg" title="Erzberg">Erzberg</a> in <a href="/wiki/Styria" title="Styria">Styria</a> furnishes high-quality iron ore for the steel industry. Crystals, such as <a href="/wiki/Cinnabar" title="Cinnabar">cinnabar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amethyst" title="Amethyst">amethyst</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, are found throughout much of the Alpine region. The cinnabar deposits in Slovenia are a notable source of cinnabar pigments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alpine crystals have been studied and collected for hundreds of years and began to be classified in the 18th century. <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> studied the shapes of crystals, and by the 19th-century crystal hunting was common in Alpine regions. David Friedrich Wiser amassed a collection of 8000 crystals that he studied and documented. In the 20th century Robert Parker wrote a well-known work about the rock crystals of the Swiss Alps; at the same period a commission was established to control and standardize the naming of Alpine minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Glaciers">Glaciers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Glaciers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_glaciers_in_Switzerland" title="List of glaciers in Switzerland">List of glaciers in Switzerland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_the_Glacier_System_of_the_Alps_by_Alexander_Keith_Johnston_1848.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="669" data-file-width="1109" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9b/Illustration_of_the_Glacier_System_of_the_Alps_by_Alexander_Keith_Johnston_1848.png/350px-Illustration_of_the_Glacier_System_of_the_Alps_by_Alexander_Keith_Johnston_1848.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9b/Illustration_of_the_Glacier_System_of_the_Alps_by_Alexander_Keith_Johnston_1848.png/525px-Illustration_of_the_Glacier_System_of_the_Alps_by_Alexander_Keith_Johnston_1848.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9b/Illustration_of_the_Glacier_System_of_the_Alps_by_Alexander_Keith_Johnston_1848.png/700px-Illustration_of_the_Glacier_System_of_the_Alps_by_Alexander_Keith_Johnston_1848.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_the_Glacier_System_of_the_Alps_by_Alexander_Keith_Johnston_1848.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This illustration of the glacier systems of the <a href="/wiki/Mont_Blanc_massif" title="Mont Blanc massif">Mont Blanc massif</a> by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Keith_Johnston_(1804%E2%80%931871)" title="Alexander Keith Johnston (1804â€“1871)">Alexander Keith Johnston</a> was first published 1848 in <i>The Physical Atlas</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> Epoch the mountains underwent severe erosion because of glaciation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerrard16_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gerrard16-43">[40]</a></sup> which was noted in the mid-19th century by naturalist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Agassiz" title="Louis Agassiz">Louis Agassiz</a> who presented a paper proclaiming the Alps were covered in ice at various intervalsâ€”a theory he formed when studying rocks near his <a href="/wiki/Neuch%C3%A2tel" title="NeuchÃ¢tel">NeuchÃ¢tel</a> home which he believed originated to the west in the Bernese Oberland. Because of his work he came to be known as the "father of the ice-age concept" although other naturalists before him put forth similar ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff63ff_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff63ff-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aletschgebiet_aus_dem_Flugzeug.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1004" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Aletschgebiet_aus_dem_Flugzeug.jpg/220px-Aletschgebiet_aus_dem_Flugzeug.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Aletschgebiet_aus_dem_Flugzeug.jpg/330px-Aletschgebiet_aus_dem_Flugzeug.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Aletschgebiet_aus_dem_Flugzeug.jpg/440px-Aletschgebiet_aus_dem_Flugzeug.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aletschgebiet_aus_dem_Flugzeug.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Louis_Agassiz" title="Louis Agassiz">Louis Agassiz</a>'s studies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unteraar_Glacier" title="Unteraar Glacier">Unteraar Glacier</a> in the 1840s showed that it moved at 100Â m (328Â ft) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff63ff_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff63ff-56">[53]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Agassiz studied glacier movement in the 1840s at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unteraar_Glacier" title="Unteraar Glacier">Unteraar Glacier</a> where he found the glacier moved 100Â m (328Â ft) per year, more rapidly in the middle than at the edges. His work was continued by other scientists and now a permanent laboratory exists inside a glacier under the <a href="/wiki/Jungfraujoch" title="Jungfraujoch">Jungfraujoch</a>, devoted exclusively to the study of Alpine glaciers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff63ff_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff63ff-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Glaciers pick up rocks and sediment with them as they flow. This causes erosion and the formation of valleys over time. The <a href="/wiki/Inn_(river)" title="Inn (river)">Inn</a> valley is an example of a valley carved by glaciers during the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">ice ages</a> with a typical terraced structure caused by erosion. Eroded rocks from the most recent ice age lie at the bottom of the valley while the top of the valley consists of erosion from earlier ice ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff63ff_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff63ff-56">[53]</a></sup> Glacial valleys have characteristically steep walls (reliefs); valleys with lower reliefs and <a href="/wiki/Scree" title="Scree">talus slopes</a> are remnants of glacial troughs or previously infilled valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerrard132_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerrard132-57">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">Moraines</a>, piles of rock picked up during the movement of the glacier, accumulate at edges, centre, and the terminus of glaciers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff63ff_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff63ff-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jungfraujoch_img_3699.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Jungfraujoch_img_3699.jpg/220px-Jungfraujoch_img_3699.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Jungfraujoch_img_3699.jpg/330px-Jungfraujoch_img_3699.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Jungfraujoch_img_3699.jpg/440px-Jungfraujoch_img_3699.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jungfraujoch_img_3699.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Sphinx Tunnel connecting <a href="/wiki/Jungfraujoch_railway_station" title="Jungfraujoch railway station">Jungfraujoch railway station</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Sphinx_Observatory" title="Sphinx Observatory">Sphinx Observatory</a>, through a glacier at the <a href="/wiki/Jungfraujoch" title="Jungfraujoch">Jungfraujoch</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Alpine glaciers can be straight rivers of ice, long sweeping rivers, spread in a fan-like shape (Piedmont glaciers), and curtains of ice that hang from vertical slopes of the mountain peaks. The stress of the movement causes the ice to break and crack loudly, perhaps explaining why the mountains were believed to be home to dragons in the medieval period. The cracking creates unpredictable and dangerous <a href="/wiki/Crevasse" title="Crevasse">crevasses</a>, often invisible under new snowfall, which causes the greatest danger to mountaineers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff71ff_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff71ff-58">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Glaciers end in ice caves (the <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne_Glacier" title="RhÃ´ne Glacier">RhÃ´ne Glacier</a>), by trailing into a lake or river, or by shedding snowmelt on a meadow. Sometimes a piece of glacier will detach or break resulting in flooding, property damage, and loss of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff71ff_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff71ff-58">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>High levels of precipitation cause the glaciers to descend to <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> levels in some areas whereas in other, more arid regions, glaciers remain above about the 3,500Â m (11,483Â ft) level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerrard78_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerrard78-59">[56]</a></sup> The 1,817Â km<sup>2</sup> (702Â sqÂ mi) of the Alps covered by glaciers in 1876 had shrunk to 1,342Â km<sup>2</sup> (518Â sqÂ mi) by 1973, resulting in decreased river run-off levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerrard108_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerrard108-60">[57]</a></sup> Forty percent of the glaciation in Austria has disappeared since 1850, and 30% of that in Switzerland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivers_and_lakes">Rivers and lakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rivers and lakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_valleys_of_the_Alps" title="List of valleys of the Alps">List of valleys of the Alps</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:St.Bartholom%C3%A4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/St.Bartholom%C3%A4.jpg/220px-St.Bartholom%C3%A4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/St.Bartholom%C3%A4.jpg/330px-St.Bartholom%C3%A4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/St.Bartholom%C3%A4.jpg/440px-St.Bartholom%C3%A4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:St.Bartholom%C3%A4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/St._Bartholomew%27s_Church,_Berchtesgaden" title="St. Bartholomew's Church, Berchtesgaden">St. Bartholomew's chapel</a> on the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigssee" title="KÃ¶nigssee">KÃ¶nigssee</a> in Bavaria is a popular tourist destination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Alps provide lowland Europe with drinking water, irrigation, and hydroelectric power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> Although the area is only about 11% of the surface area of Europe, the Alps provide up to 90% of water to lowland Europe, particularly to arid areas and during the summer months. Cities such as Milan depend on 80% of water from Alpine runoff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatrÃ©9_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChatrÃ©9-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benniston_et_al._2011,_1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benniston_et_al._2011,_1-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup> Water from the rivers is used in at least 550 <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a> power plants, considering only those producing at least 10MW of electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Major European rivers flow from the Alps, such as the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="RhÃ´ne">RhÃ´ne</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Inn_(river)" title="Inn (river)">Inn</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Po_(river)" title="Po (river)">Po</a>, all of which have headwaters in the Alps and flow into neighbouring countries, finally emptying into the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, the Mediterranean Sea, the Adriatic Sea and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. Other rivers such as the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> have major tributaries flowing into them that originate in the Alps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatrÃ©9_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-ChatrÃ©9-18">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The RhÃ´ne is second to the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> as a freshwater source to the Mediterranean Sea; the river begins as glacial meltwater, flows into <a href="/wiki/Lake_Geneva" title="Lake Geneva">Lake Geneva</a>, and from there to France where one of its uses is to cool nuclear power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benniston_et_al._2011,_3_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benniston_et_al._2011,_3-67">[64]</a></sup> The Rhine originates in a 30Â km<sup>2</sup> (12Â sqÂ mi) area in Switzerland and represents almost 60% of water exported from the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benniston_et_al._2011,_3_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Benniston_et_al._2011,_3-67">[64]</a></sup> Tributary valleys, some of which are complicated, channel water to the main valleys which can experience flooding during the snowmelt season when rapid runoff causes debris torrents and swollen rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceben_1998,_31_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ceben_1998,_31-68">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rivers form lakes, such as Lake Geneva, a crescent-shaped lake crossing the Swiss border with Lausanne on the Swiss side and the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evian-les-Bains" title="Evian-les-Bains">Evian-les-Bains</a> on the French side. In Germany, the medieval <a href="/wiki/St._Bartholomew%27s_Church,_Berchtesgaden" title="St. Bartholomew's Church, Berchtesgaden">St. Bartholomew's chapel</a> was built on the south side of the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigssee" title="KÃ¶nigssee">KÃ¶nigssee</a>, accessible only by boat or by climbing over the abutting peaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Altstadt_von_Malcesine-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Altstadt_von_Malcesine-2.jpg/220px-Altstadt_von_Malcesine-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Altstadt_von_Malcesine-2.jpg/330px-Altstadt_von_Malcesine-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Altstadt_von_Malcesine-2.jpg/440px-Altstadt_von_Malcesine-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Altstadt_von_Malcesine-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Italian_Lakes" title="Italian Lakes">Southern pre-alpine lakes</a> like <a href="/wiki/Lake_Garda" title="Lake Garda">Lake Garda</a> are characterised by warmer <a href="/wiki/Microclimate" title="Microclimate">microclimates</a> than the surrounding areas</div></div></div>
<p>Additionally, the Alps have led to the creation of large lakes in Italy. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Sarca" title="Sarca">Sarca</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Inflow_(hydrology)" title="Inflow (hydrology)">primary inflow</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Garda" title="Lake Garda">Lake Garda</a>, originates in the Italian Alps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnBrit_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-EnBrit-70">[67]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Italian_Lakes" title="Italian Lakes">Italian Lakes</a> are a popular tourist destination since the Roman Era for their mild climate.
</p><p>Scientists have been studying the <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change" title="Effects of climate change">impact of climate change</a> and water use. For example, each year more water is diverted from rivers for <a href="/wiki/Snowmaking" title="Snowmaking">snowmaking</a> in the ski resorts, the effect of which is yet unknown. Furthermore, the decrease of glaciated areas combined with a succession of winters with lower-than-expected precipitation may have a future impact on the rivers in the Alps as well as an effect on the water availability to the lowlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benniston_et_al._2011,_1_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Benniston_et_al._2011,_1-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_the_Alps" title="Climate of the Alps">Climate of the Alps</a> and <a href="/wiki/High_Alps" title="High Alps">High Alps</a></div>
<p>The Alps are a classic example of what happens when a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperateness" title="Temperateness">temperate</a> area at lower altitude gives way to higher-elevation terrain. Elevations around the world that have cold climates similar to those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar regions</a> have been called <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">Alpine</a>. A rise from sea level into the upper regions of the atmosphere causes the temperature to decrease (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate" title="Adiabatic lapse rate">adiabatic lapse rate</a>). The effect of mountain chains on prevailing winds is to carry warm air belonging to the lower region into an upper zone, where it expands in volume at the cost of a proportionate loss of temperature, often accompanied by precipitation in the form of snow or rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidgeLakeKnox1911737_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidgeLakeKnox1911737-72">[69]</a></sup> The height of the Alps is sufficient to divide the weather patterns in Europe into a wet north and dry south because moisture is sucked from the air as it flows over the high peaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aletschgletscher_mit_Pinus_cembra1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Aletschgletscher_mit_Pinus_cembra1.jpg/220px-Aletschgletscher_mit_Pinus_cembra1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Aletschgletscher_mit_Pinus_cembra1.jpg/330px-Aletschgletscher_mit_Pinus_cembra1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Aletschgletscher_mit_Pinus_cembra1.jpg/440px-Aletschgletscher_mit_Pinus_cembra1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aletschgletscher_mit_Pinus_cembra1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Aletsch_Glacier" title="Aletsch Glacier">Aletsch Glacier</a> with pine trees growing on the hillside (2007; the surface is 180Â m (590Â ft) lower than 150 years ago)</div></div></div>
<p>The severe weather in the Alps has been studied since the 18th century; particularly the weather patterns such as the seasonal <a href="/wiki/Foehn_wind" title="Foehn wind">foehn wind</a>. Numerous weather stations were placed in the mountains early in the early 20th century, providing continuous data for climatologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceben_22â€“24_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ceben_22â€“24-17">[14]</a></sup> Some of the valleys are quite arid such as the <a href="/wiki/Aosta" title="Aosta">Aosta</a> valley in Italy, the <a href="/wiki/Maurienne" title="Maurienne">Maurienne</a> in France, the <a href="/wiki/Valais" title="Valais">Valais</a> in Switzerland, and northern <a href="/wiki/Tyrol_(state)" title="Tyrol (state)">Tyrol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceben_22â€“24_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ceben_22â€“24-17">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The areas that are not arid and receive high precipitation experience periodic flooding from rapid snowmelt and runoff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceben_1998,_31_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ceben_1998,_31-68">[65]</a></sup> The mean precipitation in the Alps ranges from a low of 2,600Â mm (100Â in) per year to 3,600Â mm (140Â in) per year, with the higher levels occurring at high altitudes. At altitudes between 1,000 and 3,000Â m (3,300 and 9,800Â ft), snowfall begins in November and accumulates through to April or May when the melt begins. <a href="/wiki/Snow_line" title="Snow line">Snow lines</a> vary from 2,400 to 3,000Â m (7,900 to 9,800Â ft), above which the snow is permanent and the temperatures hover around the freezing point even during July and August. High-water levels in streams and rivers peak in June and July when the snow is still melting at the higher altitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Alps are split into five climatic zones, each with different vegetation. The climate, plant life, and animal life vary among the different sections or zones of the mountains. The lowest zone is the colline zone, which exists between 500 and 1,000Â m (1,600 and 3,300Â ft), depending on the location. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane" title="Montane">montane</a> zone extends from 800 to 1,700Â m (2,600 to 5,600Â ft), followed by the sub-Alpine zone from 1,600 to 2,400Â m (5,200 to 7,900Â ft). The Alpine zone, extending from <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a> to the snow line, is followed by the glacial zone, which covers the glaciated areas of the mountain. Climatic conditions show variances within the same zones; for example, weather conditions at the head of a mountain valley, extending directly from the peaks, are colder and more severe than those at the mouth of a valley which tend to be less severe and receive less snowfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Various models of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> have been projected into the 22nd century for the Alps, with an expectation that a trend toward increased temperatures will have an effect on snowfall, snowpack, glaciation, and river runoff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> Significant changes, of both natural and anthropogenic origins, have already been diagnosed from observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alps_conifer_and_mixed_forests" title="Alps conifer and mixed forests">Alps conifer and mixed forests</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">Flora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Flora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flora_of_the_Alps" title="Flora of the Alps">Flora of the Alps</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gentiana_clusii03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Gentiana_clusii03.jpg/220px-Gentiana_clusii03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Gentiana_clusii03.jpg/330px-Gentiana_clusii03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Gentiana_clusii03.jpg/440px-Gentiana_clusii03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gentiana_clusii03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stemless gentian (<i><a href="/wiki/Gentiana_acaulis" title="Gentiana acaulis">Gentiana acaulis</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>Thirteen thousand species of plants have been identified in the Alpine regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatrÃ©8_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChatrÃ©8-8">[5]</a></sup> Alpine plants are grouped by habitat and soil type which can be <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> or non-calcareous. The habitats range from meadows, bogs, and woodland (deciduous and coniferous) areas to soil-less scree and <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">moraines</a>, and rock faces and ridges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds43ff_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds43ff-14">[11]</a></sup> A natural vegetation limit with altitude is given by the presence of the chief <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> treesâ€”oak, beech, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ash_tree" title="Ash tree">ash</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sycamore_maple" title="Sycamore maple">sycamore maple</a>. These do not reach the same elevation, nor are they often found growing together, but their upper limit corresponds accurately enough to the change from a temperate to a colder climate that is further proved by a change in the presence of wild herbaceous vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidgeLakeKnox1911738_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidgeLakeKnox1911738-81">[78]</a></sup> This limit usually lies about 1,200Â m (3,900Â ft) above the sea on the north side of the Alps, but on the southern slopes it often rises to 1,500Â m (4,900Â ft), sometimes even to 1,700Â m (5,600Â ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Above the forestry, there is often a band of short pine trees (<i><a href="/wiki/Pinus_mugo" title="Pinus mugo">Pinus mugo</a></i>), which is in turn superseded by <i>Alpenrosen</i>, dwarf shrubs, typically <i><a href="/wiki/Rhododendron_ferrugineum" title="Rhododendron ferrugineum">Rhododendron ferrugineum</a></i> (on acid soils) or <i><a href="/wiki/Rhododendron_hirsutum" title="Rhododendron hirsutum">Rhododendron hirsutum</a></i> (on alkaline soils).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> Although Alpenrose prefers acidic soil, the plants are found throughout the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds43ff_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds43ff-14">[11]</a></sup> Above the <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a> is the area defined as "alpine" where in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpine_meadow" title="Alpine meadow">alpine meadow</a> plants are found that have adapted well to harsh conditions of cold temperatures, aridity, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-altitude_adaptation" title="High-altitude adaptation">high altitudes</a>. The alpine area fluctuates greatly because of regional fluctuations in tree lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
