<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leontopodium_alpinum1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Leontopodium_alpinum1.jpg/170px-Leontopodium_alpinum1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Leontopodium_alpinum1.jpg/255px-Leontopodium_alpinum1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Leontopodium_alpinum1.jpg/340px-Leontopodium_alpinum1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leontopodium_alpinum1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edelweiss (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edelweiss" title="Edelweiss">Leontopodium alpinum</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>Alpine plants such as the <a href="/wiki/Gentiana_acaulis" title="Gentiana acaulis">Alpine gentian</a> grow in abundance in areas such as the meadows above the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lauterbrunnental" title="Lauterbrunnental">Lauterbrunnental</a>. Gentians are named after the <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyrian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Gentius" title="Gentius">Gentius</a>, and 40 species of the early-spring blooming flower grow in the Alps, in a range of 1,500 to 2,400Â m (4,900 to 7,900Â ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> Writing about the gentians in Switzerland <a href="/wiki/D._H._Lawrence" title="D. H. Lawrence">D. H. Lawrence</a> described them as "darkening the day-time, torch-like with the smoking blueness of Pluto's gloom."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> Gentians tend to "appear" repeatedly as the spring blooming takes place at progressively later dates, moving from the lower altitude to the higher altitude meadows where the snow melts much later than in the valleys. On the highest rocky ledges, the spring flowers bloom in the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds43ff_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds43ff-14">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>At these higher altitudes, the plants tend to form isolated cushions. In the Alps, several species of flowering plants have been recorded above 4,000Â m (13,000Â ft), including <i><a href="/wiki/Ranunculus_glacialis" title="Ranunculus glacialis">Ranunculus glacialis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Androsace_alpina" title="Androsace alpina">Androsace alpina</a></i> and <i>Saxifraga biflora</i>. <i><a href="/wiki/Eritrichium_nanum" title="Eritrichium nanum">Eritrichium nanum</a></i>, commonly known as the King of the Alps, is the most elusive of the alpine flowers, growing on rocky ridges at 2,600 to 3,750Â m (8,530 to 12,300Â ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> Perhaps the best known of the alpine plants is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edelweiss" title="Edelweiss">Edelweiss</a> which grows in rocky areas and can be found at altitudes as low as 1,200Â m (3,900Â ft) and as high as 3,400Â m (11,200Â ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds43ff_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds43ff-14">[11]</a></sup> The plants that grow at the highest altitudes have adapted to conditions by specialization such as growing in rock screes that give protection from winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharp14ff_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sharp14ff-88">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The extreme and stressful climatic conditions give way to the growth of plant species with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_metabolites" title="Secondary metabolites">secondary metabolites</a> important for medicinal purposes. Origanum vulgare, Prunella vulgaris, Solanum nigrum, and <a href="/wiki/Urtica_dioica" title="Urtica dioica">Urtica dioica</a> are some of the more useful medicinal species found in the Alps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naturasollieres.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Naturasollieres.jpg/220px-Naturasollieres.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Naturasollieres.jpg/330px-Naturasollieres.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Naturasollieres.jpg/440px-Naturasollieres.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naturasollieres.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Preserved internal alpine forest and meadow, <a href="/wiki/Vanoise_National_Park" title="Vanoise National Park">Vanoise National Park</a></div></div></div>
<p>Human interference has nearly exterminated the trees in many areas, and, except for the beech forests of the Austrian Alps, forests of deciduous trees are rarely found after the extreme deforestation between the 17th and 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup> The vegetation has changed since the second half of the 20th century, as the high alpine meadows cease to be harvested for hay or used for grazing which eventually might result in a regrowth of the forest. In some areas, the modern practice of building ski runs by mechanical means has destroyed the underlying tundra from which the plant life cannot recover during the non-skiing months, whereas areas that still practice a natural <i>piste</i> type of ski slope building preserve the fragile underlayers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharp14ff_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sharp14ff-88">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna">Fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Alps are a habitat for 30,000 species of wildlife, ranging from the tiniest <a href="/wiki/Snow_flea" title="Snow flea">snow fleas</a> to <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bears</a>, many of which have made adaptations to the harsh cold conditions and high altitudes to the point that some only survive in specific micro-climates either directly above or below the <a href="/wiki/Snow_line" title="Snow line">snow line</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChatrÃ©8_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-ChatrÃ©8-8">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jeune_bouquetin_de_face.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jeune_bouquetin_de_face.jpg/170px-Jeune_bouquetin_de_face.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jeune_bouquetin_de_face.jpg/255px-Jeune_bouquetin_de_face.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jeune_bouquetin_de_face.jpg/340px-Jeune_bouquetin_de_face.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jeune_bouquetin_de_face.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young alpine ibex. When fully grown the horns of this male will be about one metre wide.</div></div></div>
<p>The largest mammal to live in the highest altitudes are the <a href="/wiki/Alpine_ibex" title="Alpine ibex">alpine ibex</a>, which have been sighted as high as 3,000Â m (9,800Â ft). The ibex live in caves and descend to eat the succulent alpine grasses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> Classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antelopes" title="Antelopes">antelopes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds43ff_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds43ff-14">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chamois" title="Chamois">chamois</a> are smaller than ibex and found throughout the Alps, living above the tree line and are common in the entire alpine range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup> Areas of the eastern Alps are still home to brown bears. In Switzerland the <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Bern" title="Canton of Bern">canton of Bern</a> was named for the bears but the last bear is recorded as having been killed in 1792 above <a href="/wiki/Kleine_Scheidegg" title="Kleine Scheidegg">Kleine Scheidegg</a> by three hunters from <a href="/wiki/Grindelwald" title="Grindelwald">Grindelwald</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many rodents such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voles" title="Voles">voles</a> live underground. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marmots" title="Marmots">Marmots</a> live almost exclusively above the tree line as high as 2,700Â m (8,900Â ft). They hibernate in large groups to provide warmth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff102ff_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff102ff-95">[92]</a></sup> and can be found in all areas of the Alps, in large colonies they build beneath the alpine pastures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds43ff_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds43ff-14">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">Golden eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bearded_vulture" title="Bearded vulture">bearded vultures</a> are the largest birds to be found in the Alps; they nest high on rocky ledges and can be found at altitudes of 2,400Â m (7,900Â ft). The most common bird is the <a href="/wiki/Alpine_chough" title="Alpine chough">alpine chough</a> which can be found scavenging at climber's huts or the <a href="/wiki/Jungfraujoch" title="Jungfraujoch">Jungfraujoch</a>, a high-altitude tourist destination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff97ff_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff97ff-96">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parnassius_phoebus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1948" data-file-width="2909" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Parnassius_phoebus.jpg/220px-Parnassius_phoebus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Parnassius_phoebus.jpg/330px-Parnassius_phoebus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Parnassius_phoebus.jpg/440px-Parnassius_phoebus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parnassius_phoebus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The alpine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_butterfly" title="Apollo butterfly">Apollo butterfly</a> has adapted to alpine conditions.</div></div></div>
<p>Reptiles such as <a href="/wiki/Viperidae" title="Viperidae">adders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viperidae" title="Viperidae">vipers</a> live up to the snow line; because they cannot bear the cold temperatures they hibernate underground and soak up the warmth on rocky ledges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff95ff_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff95ff-97">[94]</a></sup> The high-altitude <a href="/wiki/Alpine_salamander" title="Alpine salamander">Alpine salamanders</a> have adapted to living above the snow line by giving birth to fully developed young rather than laying eggs. <a href="/wiki/Brown_trout" title="Brown trout">Brown trout</a> can be found in the streams up to the snow line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff95ff_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff95ff-97">[94]</a></sup> Molluscs such as the <a href="/wiki/Helicigona" title="Helicigona">wood snail</a> live up the snow line. Popularly gathered as food, the <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a> are now protected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff88ff_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff88ff-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several species of <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moths</a> live in the Alps, some of which are believed to have evolved in the same habitat up to 120Â million years ago, long before the Alps were created. <a href="/wiki/Lycaenidae" title="Lycaenidae">Blue butterflies</a> can commonly be seen drinking from the snowmelt; some species of blues fly as high as 1,800Â m (5,900Â ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup> The butterflies tend to be large, such as those from the swallowtail <a href="/wiki/Parnassius" title="Parnassius">Parnassius</a> family, with a habitat that ranges to 1,800Â m (5,900Â ft). Twelve species of beetles have habitats up to the snow line; the most beautiful and formerly collected for its colours but now protected is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosalia_alpina" title="Rosalia alpina">Rosalia alpina</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup> Spiders, such as the large <a href="/wiki/Wolf_spider" title="Wolf spider">wolf spider</a>, live above the snow line and can be seen as high as 400Â m (1,300Â ft). <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">Scorpions</a> can be found in the Italian Alps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff88ff_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff88ff-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the species of moths and insects show evidence of having been indigenous to the area from as long ago as the Alpine orogeny. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lac_d%27Emosson" title="Lac d'Emosson">Emosson</a> in Valais, Switzerland, dinosaur tracks were found in the 1970s, dating probably from the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> Period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Alps" title="History of the Alps">History of the Alps</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_to_Christianity">Prehistory to Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Prehistory to Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scena_di_duello_R6_-_Foppe_-_Nadro_(Foto_Luca_Giarelli).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="953" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Scena_di_duello_R6_-_Foppe_-_Nadro_%28Foto_Luca_Giarelli%29.jpg/220px-Scena_di_duello_R6_-_Foppe_-_Nadro_%28Foto_Luca_Giarelli%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Scena_di_duello_R6_-_Foppe_-_Nadro_%28Foto_Luca_Giarelli%29.jpg/330px-Scena_di_duello_R6_-_Foppe_-_Nadro_%28Foto_Luca_Giarelli%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Scena_di_duello_R6_-_Foppe_-_Nadro_%28Foto_Luca_Giarelli%29.jpg/440px-Scena_di_duello_R6_-_Foppe_-_Nadro_%28Foto_Luca_Giarelli%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scena_di_duello_R6_-_Foppe_-_Nadro_(Foto_Luca_Giarelli).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pre-historic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petroglyphs" title="Petroglyphs">petroglyphs</a> from <a href="/wiki/Rock_Drawings_in_Valcamonica" title="Rock Drawings in Valcamonica">Valcamonica</a>, Italy</div></div></div>
<p>About 10,000 years ago, when the ice melted after the <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrm_glaciation" title="WÃ¼rm glaciation">WÃ¼rm glaciation</a>, late <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Palaeolithic</a> communities were established along the lake shores and in cave systems. Evidence of human habitation has been found in caves near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vercors_Cave_System" title="Vercors Cave System">Vercors</a>, close to Grenoble and <b>Echirolles</b>; in Austria, the <a href="/wiki/Mondsee_(lake)" title="Mondsee (lake)">Mondsee</a> culture shows evidence of houses built on piles to keep them dry. Standing stones have been found in the Alpine areas of France and Italy. The <a href="/wiki/Rock_Drawings_in_Valcamonica" title="Rock Drawings in Valcamonica">Rock Drawings in Valcamonica</a> are more than 5000 years old; more than 200,000 drawings and etchings have been identified at the site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beattie25ff_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beattie25ff-102">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1991, a mummy of a <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">neolithic</a> body, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi_the_Iceman" title="Ã–tzi the Iceman">Ã–tzi the Iceman</a>, was discovered by hikers on the <a href="/wiki/Similaun" title="Similaun">Similaun</a> glacier. His clothing and gear indicate that he lived in an alpine farming community, while the location and manner of his death â€“ an arrowhead was discovered in his shoulder â€“ suggests he was travelling from one place to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beattie21ff_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beattie21ff-103">[100]</a></sup> Analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> of Ã–tzi, has shown that he belongs to the K1 <a href="/wiki/Subclade" title="Subclade">subclade</a> which cannot be categorized into any of the three modern branches of that subclade. The new subclade has provisionally been named <i>K1Ã¶</i> for <i>Ã–tzi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celt" title="Celt">Celtic</a> tribes settled in Switzerland between 1500 and 1000 BC. The <a href="/wiki/Rhaetian_people" title="Rhaetian people">Raetians</a> lived in the eastern regions, while the west was occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Helvetii" title="Helvetii">Helvetii</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Allobroges" title="Allobroges">Allobrogi</a> settled in the RhÃ´ne valley and in <a href="/wiki/Savoy" title="Savoy">Savoy</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ligures" title="Ligures">Ligurians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Veneti" title="Adriatic Veneti">Adriatic Veneti</a> lived in <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Italy" title="Northwest Italy">north-west Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triveneto" title="Triveneto">Triveneto</a> respectively. Among the many substances Celtic tribes mined was salt in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Salzburg" title="Salzburg">Salzburg</a> in Austria where evidence of the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt culture</a> was found by a mine manager in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beattie25ff_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beattie25ff-102">[99]</a></sup> By the 6th century BC the <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La TÃ¨ne culture">La TÃ¨ne culture</a> was well established in the region,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup> and became known for high quality <a href="/wiki/Celtic_art#Halstatt_gallery" title="Celtic art">decorated weapons and jewellery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup> The Celts were the most widespread of the mountain tribesâ€”they had warriors that were strong, tall and fair-skinned, and skilled with iron weapons, which gave them an advantage in warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a> in 218 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Carthaginian</a> general <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> probably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hannibal_crossing_the_Alps" title="Hannibal crossing the Alps">crossed the Alps</a> with an army numbering 38,000 infantry, 8,000 cavalry, and 37 war elephants. This was one of the most celebrated achievements of any military force in ancient warfare,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancel_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lancel-108">[105]</a></sup> although no evidence exists of the actual crossing or the place of crossing. The Romans, however, had built roads along the mountain passes, which continued to be used through the medieval period to cross the mountains and Roman road markers can still be found on the mountain passes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Castle_of_Chillon_N.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2924" data-file-width="3902" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Castle_of_Chillon_N.jpg/220px-Castle_of_Chillon_N.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Castle_of_Chillon_N.jpg/330px-Castle_of_Chillon_N.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Castle_of_Chillon_N.jpg/440px-Castle_of_Chillon_N.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Castle_of_Chillon_N.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Chillon" title="ChÃ¢teau de Chillon">ChÃ¢teau de Chillon</a>, an early medieval castle on the north shore of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Geneva" title="Lake Geneva">Lake Geneva</a>, is shown here against the backdrop of the <a href="/wiki/Dents_du_Midi" title="Dents du Midi">Dents du Midi</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Roman expansion brought the defeat of the Allobrogi in 121 BC and during the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">Gallic Wars</a> in 58 BC <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> overcame the Helvetii. The Rhaetians continued to resist but were eventually conquered when the Romans turned northward to the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> valley in Austria and defeated the <a href="/wiki/Brigantes" title="Brigantes">Brigantes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beattie27ff_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beattie27ff-110">[107]</a></sup> The Romans built settlements in the Alps; towns such as <a href="/wiki/Aosta" title="Aosta">Aosta</a> (named for Augustus) in Italy, <a href="/wiki/Martigny" title="Martigny">Martigny</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lausanne" title="Lausanne">Lausanne</a> in Switzerland, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partenkirchen" title="Partenkirchen">Partenkirchen</a> in Bavaria show remains of Roman baths, villas, arenas, and temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup> Much of the Alpine region was gradually settled by <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic tribes</a>, (<a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bavarii" title="Bavarii">Bavarii</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>) from the 6th to the 13th centuries mixing with the local Celtic tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Christianity.2C_feudalism.2C_and_Napoleonic_wars"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity,_feudalism,_and_Napoleonic_wars">Christianity, feudalism, and Napoleonic wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Christianity, feudalism, and Napoleonic wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Christianity was established in the region by the Romans and saw the establishment of monasteries and churches in the high regions. The Frankish expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a> and the Bavarian expansion in the eastern Alps introduced feudalism and the building of castles to support the growing number of dukedoms and kingdoms. Castello del Buonconsiglio in <a href="/wiki/Trento" title="Trento">Trento</a>, Italy, still has intricate frescoes, excellent examples of Gothic art, in a tower room. In Switzerland, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Chillon" title="ChÃ¢teau de Chillon">ChÃ¢teau de Chillon</a> is preserved as an example of medieval architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup> There are several important alpine saints and one such one is <a href="/wiki/Saint_Maurice" title="Saint Maurice">Saint Maurice</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup> who gives his name to the town <a href="/wiki/St._Moritz" title="St. Moritz">St. Moritz</a>.
</p><p><br/>
Much of the medieval period was a time of power struggles between competing dynasties such as the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Savoy" title="House of Savoy">House of Savoy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Visconti_of_Milan" title="Visconti of Milan">Visconti</a> in northern Italy, and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">House of Habsburg</a> in Austria and Slovenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup> In 1291, to protect themselves from incursions by the Habsburgs, four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cantons" title="Cantons">cantons</a> in the middle of Switzerland drew up a <a href="/wiki/Federal_Charter_of_1291" title="Federal Charter of 1291">charter</a> that is considered to be a declaration of independence from neighbouring kingdoms. After a series of battles fought in the 13th, 14th, and 15th centuries, more cantons joined the confederacy and by the 16th century, Switzerland was well-established as a separate state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PopovAN_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/PopovAN_001.jpg/220px-PopovAN_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/PopovAN_001.jpg/330px-PopovAN_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/PopovAN_001.jpg/440px-PopovAN_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PopovAN_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian troops under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suvorov" title="Suvorov">Suvorov</a> crossing the Alps in 1799</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> in the late 18th century and early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> annexed territory formerly controlled by the Habsburgs and Savoys. In 1798, he established the Helvetic Republic in Switzerland; two years later he led an army across the St. Bernard pass and conquered almost all of the Alpine regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aussois_-_Forts_de_l%27Esseillon_-_Fort_Victor-Emmanuel,_fort_Charles-F%C3%A9lix_et_fort_Marie-Christine_-1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Aussois_-_Forts_de_l%27Esseillon_-_Fort_Victor-Emmanuel%2C_fort_Charles-F%C3%A9lix_et_fort_Marie-Christine_-1.JPG/220px-Aussois_-_Forts_de_l%27Esseillon_-_Fort_Victor-Emmanuel%2C_fort_Charles-F%C3%A9lix_et_fort_Marie-Christine_-1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Aussois_-_Forts_de_l%27Esseillon_-_Fort_Victor-Emmanuel%2C_fort_Charles-F%C3%A9lix_et_fort_Marie-Christine_-1.JPG/330px-Aussois_-_Forts_de_l%27Esseillon_-_Fort_Victor-Emmanuel%2C_fort_Charles-F%C3%A9lix_et_fort_Marie-Christine_-1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Aussois_-_Forts_de_l%27Esseillon_-_Fort_Victor-Emmanuel%2C_fort_Charles-F%C3%A9lix_et_fort_Marie-Christine_-1.JPG/440px-Aussois_-_Forts_de_l%27Esseillon_-_Fort_Victor-Emmanuel%2C_fort_Charles-F%C3%A9lix_et_fort_Marie-Christine_-1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aussois_-_Forts_de_l%27Esseillon_-_Fort_Victor-Emmanuel,_fort_Charles-F%C3%A9lix_et_fort_Marie-Christine_-1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Built from 1300 to 1500 metres high on a rock of <a href="/wiki/Quartzite" title="Quartzite">quartzite</a> and surrounded by deep cliffs, the Forts de l'Esseillon prevented any invasion.</div></div></div>
<p>After the fall of NapolÃ©on, many alpine countries developed heavy protections to prevent any new invasion. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Savoy" title="Savoy">Savoy</a> built a series of fortifications in the <a href="/wiki/Maurienne" title="Maurienne">Maurienne</a> valley to protect the major alpine passes, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Col_du_Mont-Cenis" title="Col du Mont-Cenis">col du Mont-Cenis</a> that was even crossed by <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> and his father to defeat the Lombards. The latter indeed became very popular after the construction of a paved road ordered by NapolÃ©on Bonaparte.
The BarriÃ¨re de l'Esseillon is a series of forts with heavy batteries, built on a cliff with a perfect view of the valley, a gorge on one side, and steep mountains on the other side.
</p><p>In the 19th century, the monasteries built in the high Alps during the medieval period to shelter travellers and as places of pilgrimage became tourist destinations. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benedictine" title="Benedictine">Benedictines</a> had built monasteries in <a href="/wiki/Lucerne" title="Lucerne">Lucerne</a>, Switzerland, and <a href="/wiki/Oberammergau" title="Oberammergau">Oberammergau</a>; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cistercian" title="Cistercian">Cistercians</a> in the Tyrol and at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Constance" title="Lake Constance">Lake Constance</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinians</a> had abbeys in the <a href="/wiki/Savoy" title="Savoy">Savoy</a> and one in the centre of <a href="/wiki/Interlaken" title="Interlaken">Interlaken</a>, Switzerland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Great_St_Bernard_Hospice" title="Great St Bernard Hospice">Great St Bernard Hospice</a>, built in the 9th or 10th centuries, at the summit of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Saint_Bernard_Pass" title="Great Saint Bernard Pass">Great Saint Bernard Pass</a> was a shelter for travellers and place for pilgrims since its inception; by the 19th century it became a tourist attraction with notable visitors such as author <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> and mountaineer Edward Whymper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_the_High_Alps" title="Exploration of the High Alps">Exploration of the High Alps</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Matterhorn_ascent_Dore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3617" data-file-width="2516" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Matterhorn_ascent_Dore.jpg/170px-Matterhorn_ascent_Dore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Matterhorn_ascent_Dore.jpg/255px-Matterhorn_ascent_Dore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Matterhorn_ascent_Dore.jpg/340px-Matterhorn_ascent_Dore.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Matterhorn_ascent_Dore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The <a href="/wiki/First_ascent_of_the_Matterhorn" title="First ascent of the Matterhorn">first ascent of the Matterhorn</a></i> (1865), lithograph by <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9" title="Gustave DorÃ©">Gustave DorÃ©</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon</a>-dated charcoal placed around 50,000 years ago was found in the <i>Drachloch</i> (Dragon's Hole) cave above the village of Vattis in the <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_St._Gallen" title="Canton of St. Gallen">canton of St. Gallen</a>, proving that the high peaks were visited by prehistoric people. Seven bear skulls from the cave may have been buried by the same prehistoric people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup> The peaks, however, were mostly ignored except for a few notable examples, and long left to the exclusive attention of the people of the adjoining valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidgeLakeKnox1911748_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidgeLakeKnox1911748-121">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff188ff_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff188ff-122">[119]</a></sup> The mountain peaks were seen as terrifying, the abode of dragons and demons, to the point that people blindfolded themselves to cross the Alpine passes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup> The glaciers remained a mystery and many still believed the highest areas to be inhabited by dragons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_VII_of_France" title="Charles VII of France">Charles VII of France</a> ordered his <a href="/wiki/Chamberlain_(office)" title="Chamberlain (office)">chamberlain</a> to climb <a href="/wiki/Mont_Aiguille" title="Mont Aiguille">Mont Aiguille</a> in 1356. The knight reached the summit of <a href="/wiki/Rocciamelone" title="Rocciamelone">Rocciamelone</a> where he left a bronze triptych of three crosses, a feat which he conducted with the use of ladders to traverse the ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup> In 1492, Antoine de Ville climbed Mont Aiguille, without reaching the summit, an experience he described as "horrifying and terrifying."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff188ff_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff188ff-122">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> was fascinated by variations of light in the higher altitudes, and climbed a mountainâ€”scholars are uncertain which one; some believe it may have been <a href="/wiki/Monte_Rosa" title="Monte Rosa">Monte Rosa</a>. From his description of a "blue like that of a gentian" sky it is thought that he reached a significantly high altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup> In the 18th century four <a href="/wiki/Chamonix" title="Chamonix">Chamonix</a> men almost made the summit of Mont Blanc but were overcome by altitude sickness and snowblindness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Conrad_Gessner" title="Conrad Gessner">Conrad Gessner</a> was the first naturalist to ascend the mountains in the 16th century, to study them, writing that in the mountains he found the "theatre of the Lord".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[125]</a></sup> By the 19th century more naturalists began to arrive to explore, study and conquer the high peaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleming_vii_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming_vii-129">[126]</a></sup> Two men who first explored the regions of ice and snow were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horace-B%C3%A9n%C3%A9dict_de_Saussure" title="Horace-BÃ©nÃ©dict de Saussure">Horace-BÃ©nÃ©dict de Saussure</a> (1740â€“1799) in the Pennine Alps,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> and the Benedictine monk of <a href="/wiki/Disentis" title="Disentis">Disentis</a> <a href="/wiki/Placidus_a_Spescha" title="Placidus a Spescha">Placidus a Spescha</a> (1752â€“1833).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleming_vii_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming_vii-129">[126]</a></sup> Born in Geneva, Saussure was enamoured with the mountains from an early age; he left a law career to become a naturalist and spent many years trekking through the Bernese Oberland, the Savoy, the Piedmont and Valais, studying the glaciers and geology, as he became an early proponent of the theory of rock upheaval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup> Saussure, in 1787, was a member of the third ascent of Mont Blancâ€”today the summits of all the peaks have been climbed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoumatoff197ff_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shoumatoff197ff-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Romantics_and_Alpinists">The Romantics and Alpinists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: The Romantics and Alpinists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caspar_David_Friedrich_-_Wanderer_above_the_sea_of_fog.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2980" data-file-width="2327" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Caspar_David_Friedrich_-_Wanderer_above_the_sea_of_fog.jpg/170px-Caspar_David_Friedrich_-_Wanderer_above_the_sea_of_fog.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Caspar_David_Friedrich_-_Wanderer_above_the_sea_of_fog.jpg/255px-Caspar_David_Friedrich_-_Wanderer_above_the_sea_of_fog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Caspar_David_Friedrich_-_Wanderer_above_the_sea_of_fog.jpg/340px-Caspar_David_Friedrich_-_Wanderer_above_the_sea_of_fog.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caspar_David_Friedrich_-_Wanderer_above_the_sea_of_fog.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Wanderer_above_the_Sea_of_Fog" title="Wanderer above the Sea of Fog">Wanderer above the Sea of Fog</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Caspar_David_Friedrich" title="Caspar David Friedrich">Caspar David Friedrich</a> (1818)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Albrecht_von_Haller" title="Albrecht von Haller">Albrecht von Haller</a>'s poem <i>Die Alpen</i> (1732) described the mountains as an area of mythical purity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a> was another writer who presented the Alps as a place of allure and beauty, in his novel <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julie,_or_the_New_Heloise" title="Julie, or the New Heloise">Julie, or the New Heloise</a></i> (1761), Later the first wave of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a> and <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">Turner</a> came to admire the scenery;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">Wordsworth</a> visited the area in 1790, writing of his experiences in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prelude" title="The Prelude">The Prelude</a></i> (1799). Schiller later wrote the play <i><a href="/wiki/William_Tell_(play)" title="William Tell (play)">William Tell</a></i> (1804), which tells the story of the legendary Swiss marksman <a href="/wiki/William_Tell" title="William Tell">William Tell</a> as part of the greater Swiss struggle for independence from the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">Habsburg Empire</a> in the early 14th century. At the end of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, the Alpine countries began to see an influx of poets, artists, and musicians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleming_2000,_83_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming_2000,_83-134">[131]</a></sup> as visitors came to experience the <a href="/wiki/Sublime_(philosophy)" title="Sublime (philosophy)">sublime</a> effects of monumental nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1816, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron">Byron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Percy Bysshe Shelley</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a> visited Geneva and all three were inspired by the scenery in their writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleming_2000,_83_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming_2000,_83-134">[131]</a></sup> During these visits Shelley wrote the poem "<a href="/wiki/Mont_Blanc_(poem)" title="Mont Blanc (poem)">Mont Blanc</a>", Byron wrote "<a href="/wiki/The_Prisoner_of_Chillon" title="The Prisoner of Chillon">The Prisoner of Chillon</a>" and the dramatic poem <i><a href="/wiki/Manfred" title="Manfred">Manfred</a></i>, and Mary Shelley, who found the scenery overwhelming, conceived the idea for the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i> in her villa on the shores of Lake Geneva amid a thunderstorm. When <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Coleridge</a> travelled to <a href="/wiki/Chamonix" title="Chamonix">Chamonix</a>, he declaimed, in defiance of Shelley, who had signed himself "Atheos" in the guestbook of the Hotel de Londres near Montenvers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup> "Who would be, who could be an atheist in this valley of wonders".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the mid-19th century scientists began to arrive en masse to study the geology and ecology of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the beginning of the 19th century, the tourism and mountaineering development of the Alps began. In the early years of the "<a href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_alpinism" title="Golden age of alpinism">golden age of alpinism</a>" initially scientific activities were mixed with sport, for example by the physicist <a href="/wiki/John_Tyndall" title="John Tyndall">John Tyndall</a>, with the first ascent of the Matterhorn by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Whymper" title="Edward Whymper">Edward Whymper</a> being the highlight. In the later years, the "<a href="/wiki/Silver_age_of_alpinism" title="Silver age of alpinism">silver age of alpinism</a>", the focus was on mountain sports and climbing. The first president of the <a href="/wiki/Alpine_Club_(UK)" title="Alpine Club (UK)">Alpine Club</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Ball_(naturalist)" title="John Ball (naturalist)">John Ball</a>, is considered the discoverer of the Dolomites, which for decades were the focus of climbers like <a href="/wiki/Paul_Grohmann" title="Paul Grohmann">Paul Grohmann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Innerkofler" title="Michael Innerkofler">Michael Innerkofler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Dibona" title="Angelo Dibona">Angelo Dibona</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Nazis">The Nazis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: The Nazis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ghent_altarpiece_at_Altaussee.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="814" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ghent_altarpiece_at_Altaussee.jpg/220px-Ghent_altarpiece_at_Altaussee.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ghent_altarpiece_at_Altaussee.jpg/330px-Ghent_altarpiece_at_Altaussee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ghent_altarpiece_at_Altaussee.jpg/440px-Ghent_altarpiece_at_Altaussee.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ghent_altarpiece_at_Altaussee.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Nazis hid looted art in salt mines at <a href="/wiki/Altaussee" title="Altaussee">Altaussee</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Netherlandish_art" title="Early Netherlandish art">Early Netherlandish</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ghent_Altarpiece" title="Ghent Altarpiece">Ghent Altarpiece</a></i> which sustained significant damage.</div></div></div>
<p>Austrian-born <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> had a lifelong romantic fascination with the Alps and by the 1930s established a home at <a href="/wiki/Berghof_(residence)" title="Berghof (residence)">Berghof</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Obersalzberg" title="Obersalzberg">Obersalzberg</a> region outside of <a href="/wiki/Berchtesgaden" title="Berchtesgaden">Berchtesgaden</a>. His first visit to the area was in 1923 and he maintained a strong tie there until the end of his life. At the end of World War II, the US Army occupied Obersalzberg, to prevent Hitler from retreating with the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a> into the mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1940 many of the Alpine countries were under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a>. Austria underwent a political coup that made it part of the Third Reich; France had been invaded and Italy was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fascist" title="Fascist">fascist</a> regime. Switzerland and Liechtenstein were the only countries to avoid an Axis takeover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_Confederation" title="Swiss Confederation">Swiss Confederation</a> mobilized its troopsâ€”the country follows the doctrine of "armed neutrality" with all males required to have military trainingâ€”a number that <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">General Eisenhower</a> estimated to be about 850,000. The Swiss commanders wired the infrastructure leading into the country with explosives, and threatened to destroy bridges, railway tunnels, and roads across passes in the event of a Nazi invasion; and if there was an invasion the Swiss army would then have retreated to the heart of the mountain peaks, where conditions were harsher, and a military invasion would involve difficult and protracted battles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ski_troops" title="Ski troops">Ski troops</a> were trained for the war, and battles were waged in mountainous areas such as the battle at Riva Ridge in Italy, where the American <a href="/wiki/10th_Mountain_Division" title="10th Mountain Division">10th Mountain Division</a> encountered heavy resistance in February 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup> At the end of the war, a substantial amount of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_plunder" title="Nazi plunder">Nazi plunder</a> was found stored in Austria, where Hitler had hoped to retreat as the war drew to a close. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_mines" title="Salt mines">salt mines</a> surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Altaussee" title="Altaussee">Altaussee</a> area, where American troops found 75Â kg (165Â lb) of gold coins stored in a single mine, were used to store looted art, jewels, and currency; vast quantities of looted art were found and returned to the owners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Largest_cities">Largest cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Largest cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The largest city within the Alps is the city of <a href="/wiki/Grenoble" title="Grenoble">Grenoble</a> in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. Other larger and important cities within the Alps with over 100,000 inhabitants are in <a href="/wiki/Tyrol" title="Tyrol">Tyrol</a> with <a href="/wiki/Bolzano" title="Bolzano">Bolzano</a> (<a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>), <a href="/wiki/Trento" title="Trento">Trento</a> (<a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Innsbruck" title="Innsbruck">Innsbruck</a> (<a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>). Larger cities outside the Alps are <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a> (Italy), <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> (Germany), <a href="/wiki/Graz" title="Graz">Graz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salzburg" title="Salzburg">Salzburg</a> (Austria), <a href="/wiki/Ljubljana" title="Ljubljana">Ljubljana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maribor" title="Maribor">Maribor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kranj" title="Kranj">Kranj</a> (Slovenia), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zurich" title="Zurich">Zurich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a> (Switzerland), <a href="/wiki/Nice" title="Nice">Nice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a> (France).
</p><p>Cities with over 100,000 inhabitants in the Alps are:
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable zebra">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Rank</th>
<th>Municipality</th>
<th>Inhabitants</th>
<th>Country</th>
<th>Region
</th></tr>

<td align="right">1</td>
